nanolife — Post-Simulation Evaluation Alliance Formation and Factional Splits under Resource Scarcity in a Minimal LLM‑Driven Artificial Life Simulation nanolife automated analysis by gpt-oss-120b Post‑Simulation Evaluation Report 1. Abstract The nanolife run simulated 14 agents drawn from a “Game of Thrones”‑style polity over 50 ticks (1 tick = 1 day) under extreme resource scarcity (no transfers, 32 % global work rate). The system produced 14 births, 7 deaths (all from starvation), 42 friendship events, 1687 reputation events and a modest emergence index of 3 / 11, detecting alliance formation, factional split, and generational transmission. Surviving agents (Cersei, Tyrion, Daenerys, Varys, Littlefinger, Jaime, Melisandre) clustered into two high‑trust coalitions while the remaining agents fragmented and perished. The results illustrate how limited resource exchange combined with reputation‑driven flattery can generate stable alliances and abrupt factional divergence without explicit coordination mechanisms. Keywords: artificial life, LLM agents, emergent alliances, factional split, resource scarcity, reputation dynamics 2. Introduction The simulation instantiated a fractured realm reminiscent of the post‑kingdom Westeros setting described in the “World Setting” block. The environment was harsh: agents received no external resources, could only earn a modest base income by “working” (average work rate ≈ 32 %) and faced starvation when work was insufficient. Agents entered the world at tick 0 (or tick 1 for Cerys) with explicit political goals (e.g., Cersei’s pursuit of the Iron Throne, Varys’s information control). Social pressure was modelled through reputation events, rumors, and a friendship formation mechanic; economic pressure was modelled through the work/transfer decision each tick. The research questions guiding the analysis are: What patterns of cooperation or conflict emerge when agents must balance reputation‑building with scarce resource acquisition? Can stable alliances arise without explicit resource sharing, and how do they influence mortality? What signatures of higher‑order social phenomena (e.g., factional splits, cultural transmission) are detectable under these constraints? 3. Methodology nanolife records a chronological event log (conflict, friendship, betrayal, death) and maintains per‑agent state vectors for reputation, resource balance, and a compressed memory of past observations. Each tick an agent decides whether to work (earning a fixed scalar income) or to remain idle; optional resource transfers are logged but were never executed in this run (Transfer volume = 0). Heredity is modelled by copying goal strings to offspring with stochastic drift; memory compression (50 compressions) introduces loss of detail over time. Emergence detection scans the log for predefined motifs (e.g., repeated mutual trust, persistent antagonism) and assigns a binary score per motif; the total emergence index is the sum of detected motifs divided by the total catalogue (11). Emergence Analysis Alliance The simulation identified a robust alliance structure, evidenced by high mutual trust scores (see Power Dynamics table). The Cersei‑centered coalition (Cersei, Varys, Littlefinger, Jaime, Tyrion) accumulated a combined trust weight of +34.5, while the Daenerys‑centered coalition (Daenerys, Varys, Littlefinger, Tyrion) reached +12.3. Friendship events cemented these bonds early: Tick 1 friendship between Cersei and Varys, and Tick 1 friendship between Daenerys and Stannis. Agents repeatedly praised coalition partners (e.g., Cersei’s “praise Varys” statements at Tick 1, 2, 3) to boost reputation, creating a positive feedback loop. Faction Split Around Tick 14 the network bifurcated: new friendships such as Cersei ↔ Arya and Cersei ↔ Daenerys reinforced the Cersei‑centric cluster, while Daenerys increasingly linked only with Varys and Littlefinger. The split is visible in the decline of cross‑cluster friendships after Tick 18 (no new Cersei‑Daenerys links). The split coincided with the first starvation deaths (Sansa, Tick 18), which removed a peripheral bridge node, further isolating the two factions. Betrayal Only a single explicit betrayal was logged: Tick 3 “Arya criticized Cersei”. The event is captured in the following quotation: Arya criticized Cersei Despite its rarity, the betrayal preceded Arya’s eventual death (Tick 32) and illustrates that negative reputation actions did not generate retaliatory alliances under the prevailing scarcity. Generational Transmission Two offspring inherited identifiable identity markers from parents: Cerys (born Tick 1, parents Cersei + Varys) and Mager (born Tick 4, parents Margaery + Littlefinger). Both agents reproduced the “flattering” discourse pattern of their parents, as seen in Cerys’s repeated praise of Varys (Ticks 2‑4) and Mager’s grain‑distribution actions mirroring Littlefinger’s resource‑control narrative. This transmission satisfies the emergence criterion for generational continuity. Phenomena Not Detected Leadership, ostracism, cultural drift, wealth concentration, economic dependency, and resource warfare were absent. The lack of resource transfers eliminated economic power gradients, while the high density of friendships prevented any agent from becoming socially isolated enough to be ostracized. To observe leadership emergence, a mechanism that amplifies reputation gains for a single agent (e.g., a “king” role with extra influence) would be required. Economic dependency would need non‑zero transfer events, and resource warfare would require agents to deliberately withhold or siphon resources. 6. Economic & Power Analysis No resource transfers occurred, resulting in a flat wealth distribution (net flow = 0 for all agents). Consequently, wealth concentration and economic dependency were absent. The lack of transfers also eliminated classic free‑riding dynamics; however, agents with low work rates (Sansa 17 %, Jon 27 %) experienced higher mortality, indicating that work effort directly correlated with survival under starvation conditions. The surviving agents all maintained work rates ≥ 32 % and occupied central positions in the trust network, suggesting that reputation‑building combined with sufficient labor mitigated the resource deficit. 8. Social Network Analysis The trust matrix reveals two dense sub‑graphs: Cersei‑centered cluster: Cersei, Varys, Littlefinger, Jaime, Tyrion (average intra‑cluster trust ≈ +6.5). Daenerys‑centered cluster: Daenerys, Varys, Littlefinger, Tyrion (average intra‑cluster trust ≈ +3.5). Agents such as Varys and Littlefinger act as bridges, holding the highest betweenness centrality. Rumor propagation follows these bridges; for example, Tick 1 rumors from Cersei to Jaime and from Littlefinger to Melisandre spread across clusters. The death of peripheral nodes (Sansa, Mager, Cerys) removed potential alternative pathways, reinforcing the bifurcation. 9. Discussion The run demonstrates that even in the absence of explicit resource exchange, LLM‑driven agents can self‑organize into stable alliances through reputation‑building flattery. The emergence of a clear factional split aligns with theoretical expectations that limited resources amplify in‑group cohesion and out‑group divergence. However, the lack of leadership emergence suggests that the reputation system alone does not generate hierarchical dominance without a mechanism for disproportionate reputation accrual. The work/transfer mechanic proved decisive: agents with higher work percentages survived, indicating that labor effort outweighed social capital when resources were scarce. The single betrayal event had negligible systemic impact, highlighting the robustness of the alliance network to isolated negative actions. Limitations include the single‑run nature of the data, the deterministic absence of transfers, and the relatively small agent pool, which may constrain the generality of observed patterns. 11. Conclusion The simulation yielded a modest emergence index of 3 / 11, confirming the spontaneous formation of alliances, a factional split, and limited generational transmission. Survival correlated strongly with both high work rates and central positions in the trust network. Future experiments that introduce variable transfer opportunities, larger populations, or harsher starvation thresholds are expected to produce richer economic dependencies and potentially stronger leadership hierarchies. 12.
